2.8v6.6l-1 .8.2.2.9-.7 3.4 2.5 4.5-3.6-.1-.4zm-5-1.7V8.5l.2-.1 2.2 3.5-2.4 1.5zM76.7 8l-.7.5-1.9-1.6-2.2 2 .9.9v7.5l-2.4-1.5V9.6l.8-.5-2.3-2.2-2.2 2 .9.9V17l-.3.2-2.1-1.5v-6c0-1.4-.7-1.8-1.5-2.3-.7-.5-1.1-.8-1.1-1.5 0-.6.6-.9.9-1.1v-.2c-.8 0-2.9.8-2.9 2.7 0 1 .5 1.4 1 1.9s1 .9 1 1.8v5.8l-1.1.8.2.2 1-.8 2.3 2 2.5-1.7 2.8 1.7 2 1.6 2V13c-.4-.2-.5-.5-.5-.7 0-.6.5-.8 1.3-.8h.4v6.2c-1.5.5-2.1 1.6-2.1 2.8 0 1.7 1.3 2.9 3.3 2.9 1.4 0 2.6-.2 3.8-.5 1-.2 2.3-.5 2.9-.5.8 0 1.1.4 1.1.9 0 .7-.3 1-.7 1.1v.2c1.6-.3 2.6-1.3 2.6-2.8s-1.5-2.4-3.1-2.4c-.8 0-2.5.3-3.7.5-1.4.3-2.8.5-3.2.5-.7 0-1.5-.3-1.5-1.3 0-.8.7-1.5 2.4-1.5.9 0 2 .1 3.1.4 1.2.3 2.3.6 3.3.6 1.5 0 2.8-.5 2.8-2.6V3.7l1.2-1-.2-.2zm-4.1 6.1c-.3.3-.7.6-1.2.6s-1-.3-1.2-.6V4.2l1-.7 1.4 1.3v3.8zm0 3c-.2-.2-.7-.5-1.2-.5s-1 .3-1.2.5V9c.2.2.7.5 1.2.5s1-.3 1.2-.5v2.6zm0 4.7c0 .8-.5 1.6-1.6 1.6h-.8V12c.2-.2.7-.5 1.2-.5s.9.3 1.2.5v4.3zm13.7-7.1l-3.2-2.3-4.9 2.8v6.5l-1 .8.1.2.8-.6 3.2 2.4 5-3V9.2zm-5.4 6.3V8.3l2.5 1.8v7.1l-2.5-1.7zm14.9-8.4h-.2c-.3.2-.6.4-.9.4-.4 0-.9-.2-1.1-.5h-.2l-1.7 1.9-1.7-1.9-3 2 .1.2.8-.5 1 1.1v6.3l-1.3 1 .2.2.6-.5 2.4 2 3.1-2.1-.1-.2-.9.5-1.2-1V9c.5.5 1.1 1 1.8 1 1.4.1 2.2-1.3 2.3-2.9zm12 9.6L123 19l-4.6-7 3.3-5.1h.2c.4.4 1 .8 1.7.8s1.2-.4 1.5-.8h.2c-.1 2-1.5 3.2-2.5 3.2s-1.5-.5-2.1-.8l-.3.5 5 7.4 1-.6v.1zm-11-.5l-1.3 1 .2.2.6-.5 2.2 2 3-2-.2-.2-.8.5-1-1V.8h-.1l-3.6 2.4v.2c.4.2 1 .3 1 1.5v11.3zM143 2.9c0-2-1.9-2.5-3.4-2.5v.3c.9 0 1.6.3 1.6 1 0 .4-.3 1-1.2 1-.7 0-2.2-.4-3.3-.8-1.3-.4-2.5-.8-3.5-.8-2 0-3.4 1.5-3.4 3.2 0 1.5 1.1 2 1.5 2.2l.1-.2c-.3-.2-.6-.4-.6-1 0-.4.4-1.1 1.4-1.1.9 0 2.1.4 3.7.9 1.4.4 2.9.7 3.7.8V9l-1.5 1.3v.1l1.5 1.3V16c-.8.5-1.7.6-2.5.6-1.5 0-2.8-.4-3.9-1.6l4.1-2V6l-5 2.2c.5-1.3 1.6-2.2 2.6-2.9l-.1-.2c-3 .8-5.7 3.5-5.7 6.9 0 4 3.3 7 7 7 4 0 6.6-3.2 6.6-6.5h-.2c-.6 1.3-1.5 2.5-2.6 3.1v-4.1l1.6-1.3v-.1L140 8.8v-3c1.5 0 3-1 3-2.9zm-8.7 11l-1.2.6c-.7-.9-1.1-2.1-1.1-3.8 0-.7.1-1.5.3-2.1l2.1-.9-.1 6.2zm12.2-12h-.1l-2 1.7v.1l1.7 1.9h.2l2-1.7v-.1l-1.8-1.9zm3 14.8l-.8.5-1-1V9.3l1-.7-.2-.2-.7.6-1.8-2.1-2.9 2 .2.3.7-.5.9 1.1v6.5l-1.3 1 .1.2.7-.5 2.2 2 3-2-.1-.3zm16.7-.1l-.7.5-1.1-1V9.3l1-.8-.2-.2-.8.7-2.3-2.1-3 2.1-2.3-2.1L154 9l-1.8-2.1-2.9 2 .1.3.7-.5 1 1.1v6.5l-.8.8 2.3 1.9 2.2-2-.9-.9V9.3l.9-.6 1.5 1.4v6l-.8.8 2.3 1.9 2.2-2-.9-.9V9.3l.8-.5 1.6 1.4v6l-.7.7 2.3 2.1 3.1-2.1v-.3zm8.7-1.5l-2.5 1.9-2.5-2v-1.2l4.7-3.2v-.1l-2.4-3.6-5.2 2.8v6.8l3.5 2.5 4.5-3.6-.1-.3zm-5-1.7V8.5l.2-.1 2.2 3.5-2.4 1.5zm14.1-.9l-1.9-1.5c1.3-1.1 1.8-2.6 1.8-3.6v-.6h-.2c-.2.5-.6 1-1.4 1-.8 0-1.3-.4-1.8-1L176 9.3v3.6l1.7 1.3c-1.7 1.5-2 2.5-2 3.3 0 1 .5 1.7 1.3 2l.1-.2c-.2-.2-.4-.3-.4-.8 0-.3.4-.8 1.2-.8 1 0 1.6.7 1.9 1l4.3-2.6v-3.6h-.1zm-1.1-3c-.7 1.2-2.2 2.4-3.1 3l-1.1-.9V8.1c.4 1 1.5 1.8 2.6 1.8.7 0 1.1-.1 1.6-.4zm-1.7 8c-.5-1.1-1.7-1.9-2.9-1.9-.3 0-1.1 0-1.9.5.5-.8 1.8-2.2 3.5-3.2l1.2 1 .1 3.6z"></path></svg></a></div><div class="css-o2c64h e1uwo0ec1"><a href="https://www.nytimes.com/subscriptions/Multiproduct/lp8HYKU.html?campaignId=6W74R" class="css-1kj7lfb"><button class="css-mwh6ll d="M8,10c-2.5,0-7,1.1-7,3.5V16h14v-2.5C15,11.1,10.5,10,8,10z"></path><circle cx="8" cy="4" r="4"></circle></svg></button></div></div></section><section class="hasLinks css-7ne3o6 e448j0e2"><div class="SectionBarShare-shareItem--2tuw6"></li></ul></div></div><header class="styles-headerBasic--3czk2 css-uvwqfx e111kb3g0"><div id="sponsor-wrapper" class="ResponsiveAd-emptyAd--3yMTH"><div id="sponsor-slug" class="ResponsiveAd-sponsoredByAdSlug--11gFU"><p>Supported by</p></div><div class="ad sponsor-wrapper" style="text-align:center;height:100%;display:block"><div id="sponsor"></div></div></div><p class="css-8ncjap etcg8100"><a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="/column/matter" title="Matter" target="_blank">Matter</a></p><h1 class="css-ua6igs ejekc6u0"><span>Ancestors of Modern Humans Interbred With Extinct Hominins, Study Finds</span></h1><figure class="ResponsiveMedia-media--32g1o ResponsiveMedia-toneNews--pMwMi ResponsiveMedia-sizeLarge--1k8xA ResponsiveMedia-layoutHorizontal--1e727 styles-ledeMedia--1s9BI ResponsiveMedia-toneNews--pMwMi" aria-label="media" role="group" itemProp="associatedMedia" itemID="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2016/03/18/science/22ZIMMER/22ZIMMER-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp" itemType="http://schema.org/ImageObject"><div class="ResponsiveMedia-container--G2JS6 ResponsiveMedia-toneNews--pMwMi"><span class="accessibility-visuallyHidden--OUeHR">Image</span><img alt="" class="Image-image--2zb04" src="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2016/03/18/science/22ZIMMER/22ZIMMER-articleLarge.jpg?quality=75&amp;auto=webp&amp;disable=upscale" srcSet="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2016/03/18/science/22ZIMMER/22ZIMMER-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 600w,https://static01.nyt.com/images/2016/03/18/science/22ZIMMER/22ZIMMER-jumbo.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 1024w,https://static01.nyt.com/images/2016/03/18/science/22ZIMMER/22ZIMMER-superJumbo.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 2048w" sizes="((min-width: 600px) and (max-width: 1004px)) 84vw, (min-width: 1005px) 80vw, 100vw"/></div><figcaption class="ResponsiveMedia-caption--1dUVu media-caption--3q8sa ResponsiveMedia-toneNews--pMwMi" itemProp="caption description"><span class="ResponsiveMedia-captionText--2WFdF media-captionText--1yGqw">Skulls of the Neanderthal man.</span><span class="ResponsiveMedia-credit--3F-q_ media-credit--3-06U" itemProp="copyrightHolder"><span class="accessibility-visuallyHidden--OUeHR">Credit</span><span>European Pressphoto Agency</span></span></figcaption></figure><div class="styles-bylineTimestamp--2J2fe styles-toneNews--29Vp_"><div class="css-1xhbnj5 e3y4v310"><div class="css-20okp5 e3y4v311"><p class="css-1cbhw1y e1x1pwtg1">By<!-- --> <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/by/carl-zimmer" class="css-spjqsp e1x1pwtg0">Carl Zimmer</a></p></div></div><time class="css-3u3suj eqgapgq0" dateTime="2016-03-17" itemProp="datePublished" content="2016 - 03 - 17">March 17, 2016</time></div></header><div class="StoryBodyCompanionColumn css-1bytduc emamhsk0"><div class="css-xk4wzv emamhsk2"><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">The ancestors of modern humans interbred with Neanderthals and another extinct line of humans known as the Denisovans at least four times in the course of prehistory, according to an analysis of global genomes <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="http://science.sciencemag.org/content/early/2016/03/16/science.aad9416" title="" target="_blank">published Thursday</a> in the journal Science.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">The interbreeding may have given modern humans genes that bolstered immunity to pathogens, the authors concluded.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">“This is yet another genetic nail in the coffin of our oversimplistic models of human evolution,” said Carles Lalueza-Fox, a research scientist at the Institute of Evolutionary Biology in Barcelona, Spain, who was not involved in the study.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">The new study expands on a series of findings in recent years showing that the ancestors of modern humans once shared the planet with a surprising number of near relatives — lineages like the Neanderthals and Denisovans that became extinct tens of thousands of years ago.</p></div><aside class="css-15v9f0h emamhsk1"></aside></div><div class="StoryBodyCompanionColumn css-1bytduc emamhsk0"><div class="css-xk4wzv emamhsk2"><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">Before disappearing, however, they interbred with our forebears on at least several occasions. Today, we carry DNA from these encounters.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">The first clues to ancient interbreeding surfaced in 2010, when scientists discovered that some modern humans — mostly Europeans — carried DNA that <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="http://www.nytimes.com/2010/05/07/science/07neanderthal.html" title="" target="_blank">matched material recovered from Neanderthal fossils</a>.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">Later studies showed that the forebears of modern humans <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="http://www.nytimes.com/2013/12/19/science/toe-fossil-provides-complete-neanderthal-genome.html" title="" target="_blank">first encountered Neanderthals</a> after expanding out of Africa more than 50,000 years ago.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">But the Neanderthals were not the only extinct humans that our own ancestors found. A finger bone discovered in a Siberian cave, called Denisova, <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="http://www.nytimes.com/2010/12/23/science/23ancestor.html" title="" target="_blank">yielded DNA from yet another group of humans.</a></p></div><aside class="css-15v9f0h emamhsk1"></aside></div><div class="StoryBodyCompanionColumn css-1bytduc emamhsk0"><div class="css-xk4wzv emamhsk2"><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">Research later indicated that all three groups — modern humans, Neanderthals and Denisovans — shared a common ancestor who lived roughly 600,000 years ago. And, perhaps no surprise, some ancestors of modern humans also interbred with Denisovans.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">Some of their DNA has survived in people in Melanesia, a region of the Pacific that includes New Guinea and the islands around it.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">Those initial discoveries left major questions unanswered, such as how often our ancestors interbred with Neanderthals and Denisovans. Scientists have developed new ways to study the DNA of living people to tackle these mysteries.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">Joshua M. Akey, a geneticist at the University of Washington, and his colleagues analyzed a database of 1,488 genomes from people around the world. The scientists added 35 genomes from people in New Britain and other Melanesian islands in an effort to learn more about Denisovans in particular.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">The researchers found that all of the non-Africans in their study had Neanderthal DNA, while the Africans had very little or none. That finding supported previous studies.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">But when Dr. Akey and his colleagues compared DNA from modern Europeans, East Asians and Melanesians, they found that each population carried its own distinctive mix of Neanderthal genes.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">The best explanation for these patterns, the scientists concluded, was that the ancestors of modern humans <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="http://science.sciencemag.org/lookup/doi/10.1126/science.aad9416" title="" target="_blank">acquired Neanderthal DNA on three occasions</a>.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">The first encounter happened when the common ancestor of all non-Africans interbred with Neanderthals.</p></div><aside class="css-15v9f0h emamhsk1"></aside></div><div class="StoryBodyCompanionColumn css-1bytduc emamhsk0"><div class="css-xk4wzv emamhsk2"><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">The second occurred among the ancestors of East Asians and Europeans, after the ancestors of Melanesians split off. Later, the ancestors of East Asians — but not Europeans — interbred a third time with Neanderthals.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">Earlier studies had hinted at the possibility that the forebears of modern humans had multiple encounters with Neanderthals, but hard data had been lacking.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">“A lot of people have been arguing for that, but now they’re really providing the evidence for it,” said Rasmus Nielsen, a geneticist at the University of California, Berkeley, who was not involved in the new study.</p></div><aside class="css-15v9f0h emamhsk1"></aside></div><div style="margin:0 auto" class="EmbeddedIframe-MEDIUM--2Doaq EmbeddedIframe-news--2YwnS"><div data-id="100000003899746" data-slug="human-origins-promotron" class="EmbeddedIframe-embedded--dbTIM styles-embeddedInteractive--2frSu styles-toneNews--2oQsX styles-sizeMediumInteractive--3izvz" style="max-width:600px"><iframe src="https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2017/admin/100000003899746.embedded.html?" class="Iframe-iframe--2FL4W" frameBorder="0" scrolling="no" width="100%"></iframe></div></div><div class="StoryBodyCompanionColumn css-1bytduc emamhsk0"><div class="css-xk4wzv emamhsk2"><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">The Melanesians took a different course. After a single interbreeding with Neanderthals, Dr. Akey found, their ancestors went on to interbreed just once with Denisovans as well.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">Where that encounter could have taken place remains an enigma. The only place Denisovan remains have been found is Siberia, a long way from New Guinea.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">It is possible that Denisovans ranged down to Southeast Asia, Dr. Akey said, crossing paths with modern humans who later settled in Melanesia.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">Dr. Akey and his colleagues also identified some regions of Neanderthal and Denisovan DNA that became more common in modern humans as generations passed, suggesting that they provided some kind of a survival advantage.</p></div><aside class="css-15v9f0h emamhsk1"></aside></div><div class="StoryBodyCompanionColumn css-1bytduc emamhsk0"><div class="css-xk4wzv emamhsk2"><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">Many of the regions contain immune system genes, Dr. Akey noted.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">“As modern humans are spreading out across the world, they’re encountering pathogens they haven’t experienced before,” he said. Neanderthals and Denisovans may have had genes that were adapted to fight those enemies.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">“Maybe they really helped us survive and thrive in these new environments,” he said.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">Dr. Akey and his colleagues found that Neanderthal and Denisovan DNA was glaringly absent from four regions of the modern human genome.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">That absence may signal that these stretches of the genome are instrumental in making modern humans unique. Intriguingly, one of those regions includes a gene called FOXP2, which is involved in speech.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">Scientists suspect that Neanderthals and Denisovans were not the only extinct races our ancestors interbred with.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">PingHsun Hsieh, a biologist at the University of Arizona, and his colleagues reported last month that the genomes of African pygmies contained pieces of DNA that <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="http://genome.cshlp.org/content/26/3/291.abstract" title="" target="_blank">came from an unknown source within the last 30,000 years</a>.</p><p class="css-1laz1j e2kc3sl0">Dr. Akey and his colleagues are now following up with an analysis of African populations. “This potentially allows us to find new twigs on the human family tree,” he said.</p></div><aside class="css-15v9f0h emamhsk1"></aside></div><div class="bottom-of-article"><div class="Addendum-addendums--1F9r3 Addendum-news--if0h7"></div><div
